# 🔥 CORE AWS BASICS

## ❓ Q1. What is AWS? Key services you've used?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
AWS = Amazon ka cloud platform jahan aap rent pe le sakte ho servers, storage, databases — without buying physical hardware.

**Intermediate:**  
AWS = **Collection of 200+ managed services** across compute, storage, database, networking, security, ML, IoT. Pay-as-you-go model. Global infrastructure.

**Advanced (Internal Working):**

AWS ka **Global Infrastructure** internally kaise kaam karta hai:

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                        AWS Global Network                    │
│  (Private fiber optic cables connecting all regions)        │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
        ┌─────────────────────┼─────────────────────┐
        │                     │                     │
        ▼                     ▼                     ▼
┌───────────────┐      ┌───────────────┐      ┌───────────────┐
│  Region       │      │  Region       │      │  Region       │
│  (ap-south-1) │      │  (us-east-1)  │      │  (eu-west-1)  │
│               │      │               │      │               │
│  ┌─────────┐  │      │  ┌─────────┐  │      │  ┌─────────┐  │
│  │   AZ1   │  │      │  │   AZ1   │  │      │  │   AZ1   │  │
│  │ ┌─────┐ │  │      │  │ ┌─────┐ │  │      │  │ ┌─────┐ │  │
│  │ │DC-1 │ │  │      │  │ │DC-1 │ │  │      │  │ │DC-1 │ │  │
│  │ └─────┘ │  │      │  │ └─────┘ │  │      │  │ └─────┘ │  │
│  │ ┌─────┐ │  │      │  │ ┌─────┐ │  │      │  │ ┌─────┐ │  │
│  │ │DC-2 │ │  │      │  │ │DC-2 │ │  │      │  │ │DC-2 │ │  │
│  │ └─────┘ │  │      │  │ └─────┘ │  │      │  │ └─────┘ │  │
│  └─────────┘  │      │  └─────────┘  │      │  └─────────┘  │
│  ┌─────────┐  │      │  ┌─────────┐  │      │  ┌─────────┐  │
│  │   AZ2   │  │      │  │   AZ2   │  │      │  │   AZ2   │  │
│  └─────────┘  │      │  └─────────┘  │      │  └─────────┘  │
│  ┌─────────┐  │      │  ┌─────────┐  │      │  ┌─────────┐  │
│  │   AZ3   │  │      │  │   AZ3   │  │      │  │   AZ3   │  │
│  └─────────┘  │      │  └─────────┘  │      │  └─────────┘  │
└───────────────┘      └───────────────┘      └───────────────┘
```

**Key internal concepts:**

| Concept | Explanation |
|---------|-------------|
| **Region** | Geographic area (e.g., Mumbai). Data stays inside region unless you move it. |
| **Availability Zone (AZ)** | One or more physically separate data centers within region. Connected via low-latency fiber (<1ms). |
| **Local Zone** | Extension of region closer to users (e.g., Delhi) |
| **Wavelength Zone** | Inside 5G carrier data centers (ultra-low latency) |
| **Edge Location** | CDN cache points (400+ globally) for CloudFront |

**Cloud Service Models:**

```
On-Premises (You manage everything)
     │
     ▼
IaaS (Infrastructure as a Service) → EC2, VPC, EBS
     │
     ▼
PaaS (Platform as a Service) → Elastic Beanstalk, RDS
     │
     ▼
SaaS (Software as a Service) → Gmail, Salesforce
     │
     ▼
FaaS (Function as a Service) → Lambda
```

### 2. Key Services I've Used (Deep Dive)

| Service | Category | Internal Working | Real Use Case |
|---------|----------|------------------|---------------|
| **EC2** | Compute (IaaS) | Nitro Hypervisor virtualizes hardware. Each instance gets dedicated vCPUs. EBS volumes are network-attached (up to 256k IOPS). | Hosting Spring Boot microservices |
| **S3** | Storage (Object) | Distributed hash table. Data replicated across 3+ AZs automatically. Uses REST API (HTTP). | Store user profile pictures, logs |
| **RDS** | Database (PaaS) | Managed PostgreSQL/MySQL. Aurora uses custom storage engine with 6-way replication. | Order management system |
| **Lambda** | Compute (FaaS) | Firecracker micro-VMs. Cold start = download code + start VM (500ms-2s). | Image resizing on S3 upload |
| **API Gateway** | Networking | HTTP/WebSocket proxy. Handles rate limiting, auth, request transformation. | REST API frontend |
| **DynamoDB** | Database (NoSQL) | SSD-backed, auto-sharding. Uses consistent hashing for partition distribution. | Shopping cart, session store |

### 3. Architecture / Flow Diagram

**Typical 3-Tier Architecture on AWS:**

```
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │            Route 53 (DNS)               │
                    │      (Directs traffic to nearest edge)  │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                      │
                                      ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │           CloudFront (CDN)              │
                    │      (Caches static assets globally)    │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                      │
                                      ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │           WAF (Web App Firewall)        │
                    │      (SQL injection, XSS protection)    │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                      │
                                      ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │          API Gateway / ALB              │
                    │   (Rate limiting, auth, routing)        │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                      │
                    ┌─────────────────┼─────────────────────┐
                    │                 │                     │
                    ▼                 ▼                     ▼
            ┌─────────────┐   ┌─────────────┐       ┌─────────────┐
            │   EC2-1     │   │   EC2-2     │       │   EC2-3     │
            │ (t3.large)  │   │ (t3.large)  │       │ (t3.large)  │
            │             │   │             │       │             │
            │ Spring Boot │   │ Spring Boot │       │ Spring Boot │
            └──────┬──────┘   └──────┬──────┘       └──────┬──────┘
                   │                 │                     │
                   └─────────────────┼─────────────────────┘
                                     │
                    ┌────────────────┼────────────────────┐
                    │                │                    │
                    ▼                ▼                    ▼
            ┌─────────────┐   ┌─────────────┐      ┌─────────────┐
            │  RDS        │   │ ElastiCache │      │    S3       │
            │ (Primary)   │   │  (Redis)    │      │  (Files)    │
            │ PostgreSQL  │   │  Session    │      │             │
            └──────┬──────┘   │   Cache     │      └─────────────┘
                   │          └─────────────┘
                   │ (Sync replication)
                   ▼
            ┌─────────────┐
            │  RDS        │
            │ (Standby)   │
            │ Different AZ│
            └─────────────┘
```

### 4. Step-by-Step Flow

**User request flow through AWS:**

```
1. User types api.example.com in browser
   │
   ▼
2. Route 53 → DNS resolution → Returns CloudFront edge location IP
   │
   ▼
3. CloudFront → Check cache
   ├── If cached → Return immediately
   └── If not cached → Forward to origin (ALB)
   │
   ▼
4. WAF → Inspects request (SQL injection, XSS, IP reputation)
   ├── If malicious → Block (403)
   └── If clean → Forward
   │
   ▼
5. ALB (Application Load Balancer) → 
   - Terminates SSL
   - Checks target group health
   - Routes to healthy EC2 instance (round-robin)
   │
   ▼
6. EC2 (Spring Boot) → 
   - Processes request
   - Reads from RDS / ElastiCache
   - Writes to S3 if needed
   │
   ▼
7. Response travels back same path
```

### 5. Scaling & Performance

**How AWS scales:**

| Layer | Scaling Mechanism |
|-------|-------------------|
| **DNS** | Route 53 latency-based routing + health checks |
| **CDN** | CloudFront automatically scales globally |
| **Load Balancer** | ALB automatically scales (no config needed) |
| **Compute (EC2)** | Auto Scaling Groups (add/remove based on CPU/memory) |
| **Database (RDS)** | Read replicas + Multi-AZ + Aurora auto-scaling storage |
| **Cache (ElastiCache)** | Redis cluster with sharding + replicas |

**Real numbers (my experience):**

```
EC2 Auto Scaling:
- Scale-out trigger: CPU > 70% for 2 minutes
- Scale-in trigger: CPU < 30% for 10 minutes
- Cooldown: 300 seconds
- Warm-up: 60 seconds (new instances ready)

Results:
- Handled 10,000 req/sec on 20 t3.large instances
- Scaled to 50 instances during peak (5 min)
- Cost: ~$0.05 per 1,000 requests
```

### 6. Failure Handling & Reliability

**AWS's approach to failures:**

| Failure Type | AWS Mechanism | Recovery Time |
|--------------|---------------|---------------|
| **EC2 instance crash** | Auto Scaling launches new | 2-3 minutes |
| **AZ outage** | ALB routes to other AZs | Seconds (automatic) |
| **RDS primary failure** | Multi-AZ failover | 30-60 seconds |
| **S3 regional issue** | Cross-region replication | Configurable (manual) |
| **Lambda service issue** | Automatic retry | Milliseconds |

**Design for failure principle:**

```
Instead of: "My server never fails"
Think: "My server WILL fail. How do I handle it?"

Best practices:
- Deploy across ≥2 AZs
- Use load balancers
- Implement retries with exponential backoff
- Use circuit breakers
- Have dead-letter queues for failed messages
```

### 7. Security Considerations

**Defense in depth on AWS:**

```
Layer 1: Physical Security (AWS manages)
   │
   ▼
Layer 2: Network Security
   - VPC with public/private subnets
   - Security Groups (instance firewall)
   - NACLs (subnet firewall)
   │
   ▼
Layer 3: Identity & Access
   - IAM roles (least privilege)
   - MFA for root account
   - AWS SSO for users
   │
   ▼
Layer 4: Data Security
   - Encryption at rest (EBS, S3, RDS)
   - Encryption in transit (TLS/SSL)
   - Secrets Manager for credentials
   │
   ▼
Layer 5: Monitoring & Auditing
   - CloudTrail (API audit)
   - GuardDuty (threat detection)
   - Security Hub (compliance)
```

### 8. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

❌ *"AWS is cloud provider. I use EC2, S3, RDS."* (Too basic)

✅ *"AWS provides global infrastructure with regions and AZs. In my last project, we deployed across 3 AZs in us-east-1. When one AZ had a power outage, our ALB automatically routed traffic to healthy AZs with zero downtime. We achieved 99.99% availability using Multi-AZ RDS and Auto Scaling Groups. I specifically chose RDS over DynamoDB because we needed complex joins for our reporting queries."*

**Common mistakes to avoid:**

1. Not knowing difference between region and AZ
2. Confusing IAM roles with IAM users
3. Thinking S3 is a filesystem (it's object storage)
4. Not understanding Lambda cold starts
5. Ignoring cross-AZ data transfer costs

### 9. Follow-up Questions

**Interviewer might ask:**

| Question | Expected Answer |
|----------|-----------------|
| *"How many AZs in a region?"* | Minimum 3, typically 3-6 |
| *"What's the maximum S3 object size?"* | 5TB (5GB per PUT request, use multipart upload for larger) |
| *"How does RDS Multi-AZ differ from Read Replicas?"* | Multi-AZ = synchronous replication for HA (only primary handles writes). Read Replicas = asynchronous for scaling reads. |
| *"What's a Lambda cold start?"* | First invocation after idle period. Code downloaded + VM started. Can take 500ms-2s. Solved with provisioned concurrency. |

### 10. Short Summary (Revision Notes)

```
AWS = Global cloud platform with:
- Regions (geographic, 30+)
- AZs (independent failure domains, 3-6 per region)
- Edge Locations (CDN, 400+)

Key Services:
- EC2: Virtual machines (IaaS)
- S3: Object storage (11x9 durability)
- RDS: Managed SQL (Multi-AZ for HA)
- Lambda: Serverless (pay per invocation)
- API Gateway: HTTP frontend
- DynamoDB: NoSQL (auto-scaling)

Cloud Models:
IaaS (EC2) → PaaS (RDS) → FaaS (Lambda) → SaaS

Interview Hook: 
"AWS is not just servers in cloud. It's a mindset of building 
resilient systems. I design for failure, use managed services 
to reduce ops burden, and optimize for cost and performance."
```

---

## ❓ Q2. Difference between EC2 and Lambda

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner Intuition:**

- **EC2** = Rent a computer (VM). You install OS, software, keep it running 24/7.
- **Lambda** = Give AWS your code. They run it only when needed. No server management.

**Intermediate Comparison:**

| Aspect | EC2 | Lambda |
|--------|-----|--------|
| **What you manage** | Everything (OS, patches, security, scaling) | Only code |
| **Billing** | Per hour (even idle) | Per millisecond (only during execution) |
| **Execution time** | Unlimited (years) | Max 15 minutes |
| **State** | Persistent (EBS disk) | Ephemeral (no local disk) |
| **Cold start** | Never (always on) | Yes (first invocation) |
| **Concurrency** | Limited by instances | Up to 1000 concurrent (default) |

**Advanced (Internal Working):**

**EC2 Internal Architecture:**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                  Physical Server (AWS owned)            │
│  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
│  │              Nitro Hypervisor                      │  │
│  │  (AWS custom - offloads networking, storage)      │  │
│  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
│                                                         │
│  ┌─────────────────┐    ┌─────────────────┐           │
│  │   Your VM-1     │    │   Your VM-2     │           │
│  │  (t3.micro)     │    │  (t3.large)     │           │
│  │  ┌───────────┐  │    │  ┌───────────┐  │           │
│  │  │  vCPU 1   │  │    │  │  vCPU 1   │  │           │
│  │  │  vCPU 2   │  │    │  │  vCPU 2   │  │           │
│  │  │  RAM 1GB  │  │    │  │  RAM 8GB  │  │           │
│  │  │  EBS      │  │    │  │  EBS      │  │           │
│  │  └───────────┘  │    │  └───────────┘  │           │
│  └─────────────────┘    └─────────────────┘           │
│                                                         │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │         Nitro Cards (Hardware offload)          │   │
│  │  - VPC Networking (ENC)  - EBS Storage          │   │
│  │  - Instance Monitoring   - Security             │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Key EC2 internals:**
- **Nitro System** = AWS's custom hardware that replaces traditional hypervisor
- **Dedicated vCPUs** = Your instance doesn't share CPU cores with others (no noisy neighbors)
- **EBS volumes** = Network-attached storage (not local to server)
- **Placement Groups** = Control physical proximity:

```
Placement Group Types:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ 1. Cluster: All instances in same rack (low latency,    │
│             high risk - single failure point)           │
│                                                         │
│ 2. Spread: Each instance on different rack (max 7 per  │
│            AZ) - high availability                      │
│                                                         │
│ 3. Partition: Group of racks (for large distributed    │
│               systems like HDFS, Kafka)                 │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Lambda Internal Architecture:**

```
Lambda Invocation Flow (Internal):

User Request
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              Lambda Control Plane                       │
│  (Manages worker pool, code storage, scheduling)       │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              Worker Manager                             │
│  - Maintains pool of warm execution environments       │
│  - Assigns requests to available workers               │
│  - Spins up new workers when needed                    │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│         Firecracker MicroVM (per invocation)           │
│  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
│  │  - Lightweight VMM (open source from AWS)         │  │
│  │  - Boots in <150ms                                │  │
│  │  - 5MB memory footprint (vs 1GB for traditional)  │  │
│  │  - Each Lambda gets its own MicroVM (isolated)    │  │
│  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
│                                                         │
│  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
│  │              Execution Environment                │  │
│  │  - OS: Amazon Linux 2 (micro)                     │  │
│  │  - Runtime: Node.js/Python/Java/Go/.NET/Custom   │  │
│  │  - /tmp storage: 512MB - 10GB (ephemeral)        │  │
│  │  - Memory: 128MB - 10GB (CPU scales linearly)    │  │
│  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Cold Start Deep Dive:**

```
Cold Start Timeline (Java Lambda):
─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────
Time 0ms:   Invocation received
   │
   ├─── 50ms: Worker Manager finds no warm environment
   │
   ├─── 100ms: Firecracker MicroVM starts
   │
   ├─── 200ms: OS boots (minimal kernel)
   │
   ├─── 400ms: Java runtime (JVM) starts
   │
   ├─── 800ms: Classloader loads dependencies
   │
   ├─── 1200ms: Static initializers run
   │
   ├─── 1500ms: Handler method called
   │
   ▼
Total cold start: 1-2 seconds (Java)
                   300-500ms (Node.js/Python)

Warm start (environment reused):
   ├─── 5ms: Direct handler invocation
   ▼
Total: <10ms
```

### 2. Why & When to Use

**EC2 - Use when:**

| Scenario | Why EC2? |
|----------|----------|
| 24/7 application | Pay per hour but always ready |
| Need full OS control | Custom kernel, drivers, system libraries |
| GPU workloads (ML training) | Lambda has no GPU (yet) |
| Legacy .NET Framework apps | Lambda supports only .NET Core |
| Long-running batch jobs (>15 min) | Lambda's 15-min limit |
| Windows applications | Lambda supports only Linux |
| High and steady traffic | Reserved instances = 75% cheaper than Lambda |

**Lambda - Use when:**

| Scenario | Why Lambda? |
|----------|-------------|
| Spiky/unpredictable traffic | Pay only for what you use |
| Event-driven | S3 upload, SQS message, API call |
| Short execution (<1 second ideal) | Low latency for sync APIs |
| Infrequent tasks | Scheduled jobs (once per day) |
| Real-time file processing | Image resize, video transcode |
| Data transformation | ETL pipelines, Kinesis stream processing |

**Real-world hybrid example:**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  E-commerce Order Processing System                         │
│                                                             │
│  API Gateway + Lambda (sync)                               │
│  └─> Validates order, writes to SQS (<100ms)               │
│                                                             │
│  SQS Queue                                                  │
│  └─> Buffers orders during peak                            │
│                                                             │
│  EC2 Auto Scaling Group (async workers)                    │
│  └─> Processes orders (takes 30-60 seconds)                │
│      - Inventory check                                      │
│      - Payment processing (3rd party API)                   │
│      - Email confirmation                                   │
│      - Warehouse notification                               │
│                                                             │
│  Why Lambda for API? → Spiky traffic, low latency needed    │
│  Why EC2 for processing? → Long-running, steady workload    │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 3. Trade-offs Analysis

| Factor | EC2 | Lambda | Winner |
|--------|-----|--------|--------|
| **Cost at 10 req/day** | $15/month (t3.micro) | $0.001/month | Lambda |
| **Cost at 10M req/day** | $500/month (reserved) | $5000/month | EC2 |
| **Cold start** | None | 500ms-2s (Java) | EC2 |
| **Operational effort** | High (patching, AMIs) | Zero | Lambda |
| **Max execution** | Unlimited | 15 min | EC2 |
| **State management** | Easy (EBS) | Hard (external DB) | EC2 |
| **Auto-scaling speed** | 2-5 min | Milliseconds | Lambda |
| **VPC access** | Yes (native) | Yes (with ENI, slower cold start) | EC2 |

### 4. Scaling & Performance

**EC2 Auto Scaling Deep Dive:**

```
Auto Scaling Configuration:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ Auto Scaling Group (ASG)                                   │
│                                                             │
│ Launch Template:                                           │
│   - AMI: ami-12345 (pre-baked with app)                   │
│   - Instance type: t3.large                               │
│   - Security groups, IAM role, user data                  │
│                                                             │
│ Scaling Policies:                                          │
│   1. Target Tracking: CPU @ 70%                           │
│      - Adds instances when CPU > 70% for 2 min            │
│      - Removes when CPU < 30% for 10 min                  │
│                                                             │
│   2. Scheduled: Scale to 10 instances at 9 AM (peak)      │
│                                                             │
│   3. Simple: SQS queue depth > 1000 → add 2 instances     │
│                                                             │
│ Warm-up: 60 seconds (new instances ready)                 │
│ Cool-down: 300 seconds (avoid rapid scaling)              │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Lambda Scaling Deep Dive:**

```
Lambda Concurrency Model:

Request 1 ──→ ┌─────────┐
Request 2 ──→ │Lambda-1 │ (warm)
Request 3 ──→ │Lambda-2 │ (warm)
Request 4 ──→ │Lambda-3 │ (cold start)
Request 5 ──→ │Lambda-4 │ (cold start)
   ...        └─────────┘

Concurrency Limits:
- Default: 1000 per region (all functions combined)
- Burst: 500-3000 per minute (depending on region)
- Reserved concurrency: Guarantee for critical functions
- Provisioned concurrency: Keep X instances warm

Throttling behavior:
- Sync invocation → 429 Too Many Requests
- Async invocation → Retry with exponential backoff (then DLQ)
```

### 5. Failure Handling

**EC2 Failures:**

| Failure | Detection | Recovery |
|---------|-----------|----------|
| Instance crash | EC2 health check (5 min) | ASG launches new (2-3 min) |
| AZ outage | ALB health check (30 sec) | ALB routes to other AZs |
| EBS volume failure | CloudWatch alarm | Restore from snapshot |
| OS corruption | Manual recovery | Replace instance |

**Lambda Failures:**

| Failure | Recovery |
|---------|----------|
| Function code error | Retry based on invocation type |
| Timeout (15 min) | Function fails, no retry for sync |
| Service outage | AWS handles internally |
| Out of memory | Function fails, increase memory |

### 6. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"For my last project, we built a document processing pipeline. The upload API used API Gateway + Lambda (sync, <100ms) because traffic was bursty. But the PDF generation (5-10 minutes) ran on EC2 with Auto Scaling. We used SQS between them. This hybrid approach saved 70% cost compared to all-EC2 and had zero cold starts compared to all-Lambda."*

**Common mistakes:**

- Saying Lambda is "always cheaper" (false for high traffic)
- Not knowing Lambda's 15-minute limit
- Thinking EC2 is "set and forget" (needs patching, AMI management)
- Ignoring Lambda cold starts in latency-sensitive APIs

### 7. Follow-up Questions

| Question | Answer |
|----------|--------|
| *"How do you handle Lambda cold starts for critical APIs?"* | Provisioned concurrency (keep functions warm) |
| *"What's the maximum Lambda memory?"* | 10GB (CPU scales with memory) |
| *"How do you run a 30-minute job on Lambda?"* | You don't. Use ECS/Fargate or Step Functions |
| *"What's an EC2 Spot instance?"* | Unused capacity at 90% discount, can be terminated anytime |

### 8. Short Summary

```
EC2 vs Lambda Quick Decision Tree:

Question 1: Is workload 24/7?
   YES → EC2 (reserved instances)
   NO  → Continue

Question 2: Does it run >15 minutes?
   YES → EC2 or ECS
   NO  → Continue

Question 3: Need GPU or Windows?
   YES → EC2
   NO  → Lambda

Question 4: Traffic pattern?
   Steady → EC2
   Spiky  → Lambda

Pro Tip: Use BOTH!
- API frontend: Lambda (spiky)
- Background workers: EC2 (steady)
- SQS to decouple
```

---

## ❓ Q3. What is S3? Use cases?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
S3 = Simple Storage Service. Like Google Drive but for developers. Store and retrieve any amount of data from anywhere.

**Intermediate:**  
S3 = **Object storage** (not file system). Data stored as objects (file + metadata) in buckets. Each object has a unique key. Accessed via REST API.

**Advanced (Internal Working):**

```
S3 Internal Architecture:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    S3 Request Router                        │
│         (Global namespace: bucket-name.s3.amazonaws.com)    │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
                              ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              Partition Layer (Consistent Hashing)           │
│  - Maps bucket + key to partition                          │
│  - Partitions split automatically when too large           │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
                              ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                  Storage Nodes (3+ AZs)                     │
│                                                             │
│   AZ-1              AZ-2              AZ-3                 │
│  ┌─────┐          ┌─────┐          ┌─────┐                │
│  │Node │◄────────►│Node │◄────────►│Node │                │
│  │     │  Quorum  │     │  Quorum  │     │                │
│  │Copy1│  writes  │Copy2│  writes  │Copy3│                │
│  └─────┘          └─────┘          └─────┘                │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Key Internal Concepts:**

| Concept | Explanation |
|---------|-------------|
| **Consistent Hashing** | S3 uses a distributed hash table to locate objects. Bucket+key hashed to determine partition. |
| **Quorum Writes** | Write must succeed on at least 2 of 3 AZs before returning success. |
| **Read-after-write consistency** | For new objects (PUT). For overwrites, eventual consistency (typically <1 sec). |
| **Durability** | 99.999999999% (11 nines). Lose 1 object per 10,000 years. |
| **Availability** | 99.99% (S3 Standard). 4.38 hours downtime per year. |

### 2. Storage Classes (Deep Dive)

```
S3 Storage Classes (Cost vs Access):

        Cost ($/GB/month)
              ↑
              │
$0.023       │ ● S3 Standard (frequent access)
              │
$0.0125      │ ● S3 Intelligent-Tiering (auto moves)
              │
$0.010       │ ● S3 Standard-IA (infrequent access)
              │
$0.004       │ ● S3 One Zone-IA (single AZ)
              │
$0.00099     │ ● S3 Glacier Instant (archive, ms access)
              │
$0.00036     │ ● S3 Glacier Flexible (1-5 min retrieval)
              │
$0.00018     │ ● S3 Glacier Deep Archive (12 hour retrieval)
              │
              └────────────────────────────────────→ Access Frequency
```

**When to use each:**

| Storage Class | Use Case | Retrieval | Min Storage |
|---------------|----------|-----------|-------------|
| **Standard** | Active data, frequently accessed | Millisecond | None |
| **Intelligent-Tiering** | Unknown access patterns | Millisecond | 30 days |
| **Standard-IA** | Long-lived, infrequent access (backups) | Millisecond | 30 days |
| **One Zone-IA** | Re-creatable data (thumbnails) | Millisecond | 30 days |
| **Glacier Instant** | Archive with instant access | Millisecond | 90 days |
| **Glacier Flexible** | Archive, 1-5 min retrieval | 1-5 min | 90 days |
| **Glacier Deep** | Compliance, 12-hour retrieval | 12 hours | 180 days |

### 3. Use Cases (Real-World)

**Use Case 1: Static Website Hosting**

```
Architecture:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  S3 Bucket: my-website.com                                 │
│  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐ │
│  │  index.html                                           │ │
│  │  styles.css                                           │ │
│  │  app.js                                               │ │
│  │  images/logo.png                                      │ │
│  │  videos/demo.mp4                                      │ │
│  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘ │
│                                                             │
│  Static website hosting: Enabled                           │
│  Public access: Yes (read-only)                            │
│  Bucket policy: Allow GetObject for principals              │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
                              ▼
                    ┌─────────────────┐
                    │  CloudFront     │
                    │  (CDN + HTTPS)  │
                    └─────────────────┘
                              │
                              ▼
                          User Browser

Cost: ~$0.50/month for low-traffic site
```

**Use Case 2: User Uploaded Files**

```
Flow:
User uploads profile picture
     │
     ▼
API Gateway + Lambda (resize image)
     │
     ▼
S3 bucket: user-uploads/profiles/user123/avatar.jpg
     │
     ▼
S3 Event Notification triggers Lambda
     │
     ▼
Lambda creates thumbnail (200x200)
     │
     ▼
Saves to: user-uploads/thumbnails/user123/avatar-thumb.jpg

Security:
- Pre-signed URLs (temporary access)
- Server-side encryption (SSE-S3 or SSE-KMS)
- Lifecycle policy: Delete after 30 days
```

**Use Case 3: Application Logs**

```
Log aggregation pattern:

EC2 Instances ──→ CloudWatch Agent ──→ S3 (log archive)
     │
     │ (or direct)
     │
     └──→ S3 (batch every 5 minutes)

S3 bucket structure:
logs/
  year=2024/
    month=01/
      day=15/
        hour=10/
          app-logs-10-15-2024-10-30.json

Benefits:
- Infinite retention
- Athena queries directly on S3 (no ETL)
- Lifecycle: After 30 days → Glacier
- After 1 year → Delete
```

**Use Case 4: Data Lake for Analytics**

```
Data Lake Architecture:

Real-time data (Kinesis) ──→ S3 (raw zone)
                              │
                              ▼
                     ETL (Glue/Spark)
                              │
                              ▼
                         S3 (curated zone)
                              │
                              ▼
                 ┌────────────┼────────────┐
                 │            │            │
                 ▼            ▼            ▼
             Athena       Redshift     QuickSight
            (SQL)        (DW)         (BI)

Benefits:
- Schema-on-read (don't need to predefine)
- Pay only for queries run
- Unlimited scale
```

### 4. S3 Performance Optimization

**Key Performance Limits:**

| Operation | Baseline | With Prefix | Maximum |
|-----------|----------|-------------|---------|
| PUT/LIST/DELETE | 3,500 req/sec | 5,500 req/sec | 5,500 |
| GET/HEAD | 5,500 req/sec | 11,000 req/sec | 11,000 |

**Performance Optimization Techniques:**

```
1. Key Naming for High Throughput:
   
   BAD (sequential IDs): 
   logs/2024-01-15-10-00-00.log
   logs/2024-01-15-10-00-01.log
   (All in same partition → bottleneck)

   GOOD (random prefixes):
   logs/a/2024-01-15-10-00-00.log
   logs/b/2024-01-15-10-00-01.log
   logs/c/2024-01-15-10-00-02.log
   (Spreads across partitions → parallel)

2. Multipart Upload for large files (>100MB):
   - Break file into parts (5MB - 5GB)
   - Upload parts in parallel
   - Assemble on S3
   - Can resume failed uploads

3. S3 Transfer Acceleration:
   - AWS edge locations globally
   - Upload to edge → AWS backbone → S3
   - Best for cross-region uploads
```

### 5. Data Consistency Model

```
S3 Consistency Behavior:

┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                     WRITE OPERATIONS                        │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│ PUT new object:                                            │
│   - Read-after-write consistency (immediate)               │
│   - Example: PUT key X → GET key X → returns new value     │
│                                                             │
│ UPDATE existing object:                                    │
│   - Eventual consistency (typically <1 sec)                │
│   - Example: PUT key X (v2) → GET key X → may return v1    │
│                                                             │
│ DELETE object:                                             │
│   - Eventual consistency                                    │
│   - GET after DELETE → may still get object                │
│                                                             │
│ LIST objects after PUT:                                    │
│   - Eventual consistency                                    │
│   - New object may not appear immediately                  │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Solution for strong consistency:
- Use DynamoDB for read-after-write on updates
- Versioning + GET by version ID
- S3 Select (single object queries)
```

### 6. Security Deep Dive

```
S3 Security Layers:

Layer 1: Bucket Policies (IAM)
{
  "Version": "2012-10-17",
  "Statement": [
    {
      "Effect": "Allow",
      "Principal": {"AWS": "arn:aws:iam::123456789012:role/MyAppRole"},
      "Action": "s3:GetObject",
      "Resource": "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket/private/*",
      "Condition": {
        "IpAddress": {"aws:SourceIp": "10.0.0.0/8"},
        "Bool": {"aws:SecureTransport": "true"}  // HTTPS only
      }
    }
  ]
}

Layer 2: Pre-signed URLs (temporary access)
URL expires in 5 minutes:
https://my-bucket.s3.amazonaws.com/file.pdf?
X-Amz-Algorithm=AWS4-HMAC-SHA256&
X-Amz-Credential=...&
X-Amz-Expires=300&
X-Amz-Signature=...

Layer 3: Encryption
- SSE-S3: S3 manages keys (free)
- SSE-KMS: AWS KMS (audit trail, separate permission)
- SSE-C: Customer provides keys (S3 doesn't store)
- Client-side: Encrypt before upload

Layer 4: Access Logging
- Server access logs (to another bucket)
- CloudTrail (API calls)
- VPC endpoints (no internet traversal)
```

### 7. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"S3 is an object store, not a file system. In my last project, we stored user profile pictures. We used S3 with CloudFront CDN for global delivery. For security, we used pre-signed URLs that expire in 5 minutes. We also set lifecycle policies to move older files to Glacier after 30 days, saving 70% on storage costs. When a user uploads a file, S3 triggers a Lambda to create thumbnails and run virus scan."*

**Common mistakes:**

- Thinking S3 is a filesystem (no seek, no rename)
- Not understanding eventual consistency
- Making buckets public when should use pre-signed URLs
- Not using multipart upload for large files
- Ignoring request rate limits

### 8. Follow-up Questions

| Question | Answer |
|----------|--------|
| *"How do you rename a folder in S3?"* | You can't. Folders are a UI concept. Rename = copy each object with new prefix + delete old. |
| *"What's the max S3 object size?"* | 5TB. Max PUT size is 5GB (use multipart for >5GB). |
| *"How do you ensure S3 objects are deleted after 1 year?"* | Lifecycle policy with Expiration action. |
| *"Can you run SQL on S3?"* | Yes, S3 Select (on single object) or Athena (on many objects). |

### 9. Short Summary

```
S3 = Object storage (not file system)
- Buckets (containers) + Objects (files) + Keys (paths)
- 11 nines durability (replicated across 3+ AZs)
- Eventual consistency for updates/deletes

Key Features:
- Static website hosting
- Versioning (recover deleted objects)
- Lifecycle policies (auto tiering)
- Event notifications (SQS, SNS, Lambda)
- Pre-signed URLs (temporary access)

Performance:
- 3,500 PUT/5,500 GET per second per prefix
- Use random prefixes for high throughput
- Multipart upload for >100MB

Storage Classes:
Hot → Standard (frequent)
Warm → IA, Intelligent (infrequent)
Cold → Glacier, Deep Archive (archive)

Interview Hook: 
"S3 is infinitely scalable object storage. I've used it for 
static hosting, logs, backups, and data lakes. The key is 
understanding its eventual consistency and choosing the 
right storage class for cost optimization."
```

---

# 🔥 CORE AWS BASICS (Continued)

## ❓ Q4. What is IAM? Role vs Policy vs User

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
IAM = Identity and Access Management. Control **who** can do **what** on **which AWS resources**.

**Intermediate:**  
IAM is AWS's **authentication** (who are you?) and **authorization** (what can you do?) service. It's global (not region-specific).

**Advanced (Internal Working):**

```
IAM Architecture:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    IAM Control Plane                        │
│                 (Global service, replicated)                │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
                              │
        ┌─────────────────────┼─────────────────────┐
        │                     │                     │
        ▼                     ▼                     ▼
┌───────────────┐     ┌───────────────┐     ┌───────────────┐
│ Authentication│     │ Authorization │     │   Auditing    │
│  - Sign-in    │     │  - Policy     │     │  - CloudTrail │
│  - MFA        │     │    evaluation │     │  - Last       │
│  - SAML/SSO   │     │  - Boundary   │     │    accessed   │
└───────────────┘     └───────────────┘     └───────────────┘
```

**How Authorization Works (Internal):**

```
User request to access S3 bucket
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  Step 1: Authentication Check                              │
│  - Is the requester authenticated? (has valid credentials) │
│  - If no → Return 401 Unauthorized                         │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  Step 2: Gather all applicable policies                    │
│  - Identity-based (User, Group, Role)                      │
│  - Resource-based (Bucket policy, SQS queue policy)        │
│  - IAM boundaries (limits max permissions)                 │
│  - Session policies (for temporary credentials)            │
│  - Service control policies (from AWS Organizations)       │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
     │
     ▼
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  Step 3: Policy Evaluation (Order matters!)                │
│                                                             │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │ 1. Is there an explicit DENY? → YES → DENY access   │   │
│  │ 2. Is there an explicit ALLOW? → YES → ALLOW access │   │
│  │ 3. Otherwise → DENY (implicit deny)                 │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                             │
│  IMPORTANT: Explicit DENY overrides any ALLOW!              │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 2. IAM User vs Role vs Policy (Deep Dive)

**IAM User:**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                        IAM User                             │
│                                                             │
│  Represents: A person or application (long-term identity)  │
│                                                             │
│  Credentials:                                              │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │ Console access: Username + Password + MFA           │   │
│  │ Programmatic: Access Key ID + Secret Access Key     │   │
│  │ (NEVER share these - rotate regularly)              │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                             │
│  Best Practices:                                           │
│  - Never use root user (except for account setup)          │
│  - Create individual users (not shared)                    │
│  - Apply least privilege (minimum permissions)             │
│  - Enable MFA (especially for privileged users)            │
│  - Delete unused credentials                               │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**IAM Role:**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                        IAM Role                             │
│                                                             │
│  Represents: An identity that can be ASSUMED (not a person)│
│                                                             │
│  Key Characteristics:                                      │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │ - No long-term credentials (password/access keys)   │   │
│  │ - Temporary credentials via AWS STS                 │   │
│  │ - Credentials expire (15 min - 36 hours)            │   │
│  │ - Can be assumed by: Users, Services, AWS accounts  │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                             │
│  Trust Policy (WHO can assume this role):                  │
│  {                                                         │
│    "Effect": "Allow",                                      │
│    "Principal": {                                          │
│      "Service": "ec2.amazonaws.com"  // EC2 can assume     │
│    },                                                      │
│    "Action": "sts:AssumeRole"                              │
│  }                                                         │
│                                                             │
│  Permission Policy (WHAT can they do):                     │
│  {                                                         │
│    "Effect": "Allow",                                      │
│    "Action": ["s3:GetObject", "s3:PutObject"],            │
│    "Resource": "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket/*"                  │
│  }                                                         │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**IAM Policy:**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                      IAM Policy (JSON)                      │
│                                                             │
│  Structure:                                                │
│  {                                                         │
│    "Version": "2012-10-17",    // Policy language version │
│    "Id": "S3ReadOnlyPolicy",   // Optional identifier     │
│    "Statement": [              // One or more statements  │
│      {                                                    │
│        "Sid": "AllowReadAccess",   // Statement ID        │
│        "Effect": "Allow",          // Allow or Deny       │
│        "Principal": {...},         // WHO (for resource-  │
│                                    // based policies)     │
│        "Action": [                 // WHAT actions        │
│          "s3:GetObject",                                   │
│          "s3:ListBucket"                                   │
│        ],                                                 │
│        "Resource": [              // WHICH resources      │
│          "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket",                        │
│          "arn:aws:s3:::my-bucket/*"                       │
│        ],                                                 │
│        "Condition": {             // WHEN (optional)      │
│          "IpAddress": {                                   │
│            "aws:SourceIp": "203.0.113.0/24"               │
│          },                                               │
│          "Bool": {                                        │
│            "aws:SecureTransport": "true"  // HTTPS only   │
│          }                                                │
│        }                                                  │
│      }                                                    │
│    ]                                                      │
│  }                                                        │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 3. IAM Groups (Important but often forgotten)

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                      IAM Groups                             │
│                                                             │
│  Purpose: Manage permissions for multiple users together   │
│                                                             │
│  Example Group Structure:                                  │
│                                                             │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │ Group: developers                                    │   │
│  │ Policies:                                           │   │
│  │   - EC2FullAccess                                   │   │
│  │   - S3ReadOnly                                      │   │
│  │   - CloudWatchFullAccess                            │   │
│  │                                                     │   │
│  │ Members: alice, bob, charlie                        │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                             │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │ Group: admins                                       │   │
│  │ Policies:                                           │   │
│  │   - AdministratorAccess                             │   │
│  │                                                     │   │
│  │ Members: alice (only)                               │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                             │
│  Best Practice: Assign permissions via GROUPS, not users   │
│  - New employee joins developers group → gets permissions  │
│  - No need to update individual user policies              │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 4. Common IAM Patterns (Real-World)

**Pattern 1: EC2 Instance Profile (Most Common)**

```
Scenario: EC2 needs to read from S3 and write to DynamoDB

Step 1: Create IAM Role
{
  "Trust Policy": {
    "Principal": { "Service": "ec2.amazonaws.com" }
  },
  "Permissions": [
    "s3:GetObject",
    "dynamodb:PutItem"
  ]
}

Step 2: Launch EC2 with this IAM Role
- Console: Select "IAM Role" during launch
- CLI: --iam-instance-profile Name=MyAppRole

Step 3: Application code (no credentials needed!)
// AWS SDK automatically gets temporary credentials from metadata service
s3Client.getObject(...);  // Works!

✅ No hardcoded keys
✅ Automatic rotation (every 6 hours)
✅ Can't leak credentials
```

**Pattern 2: Cross-Account Access**

```
Scenario: Account A needs to read S3 bucket in Account B

Account B (bucket owner):
- Create IAM Role "AllowReadFromAccountA"
- Trust policy: Allow Account A's root/user to assume
- Permission policy: s3:GetObject on specific bucket

Account A (requester):
- User/role has "sts:AssumeRole" permission
- Call: assumeRole(role-arn-in-account-B)
- Get temporary credentials
- Use to access Account B's bucket
```

**Pattern 3: Temporary Credentials for Mobile App**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│  Mobile App Authentication Flow                             │
│                                                             │
│  1. User logs in (via Cognito/Facebook/Google)             │
│                    │                                        │
│                    ▼                                        │
│  2. Cognito Identity Pool exchanges for AWS credentials    │
│                    │                                        │
│                    ▼                                        │
│  3. IAM Role assigned with limited permissions             │
│     - Can upload to S3: user/{user-id}/*                    │
│     - Cannot read other users' data                        │
│                    │                                        │
│                    ▼                                        │
│  4. Mobile app uses temporary credentials (1 hour expiry)  │
│     - Directly upload to S3 (no backend)                   │
│     - Secure, scalable, cheap                              │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 5. IAM Best Practices (Interview Gold)

```
IAM Best Practices Checklist:

□ 1. Use IAM roles for EC2/Lambda (never store keys on instances)

□ 2. Enable MFA for all users (especially root and admins)

□ 3. Rotate access keys regularly (every 90 days)

□ 4. Delete unused IAM users and keys

□ 5. Use IAM groups (assign permissions to groups, not users)

□ 6. Apply least privilege (start with minimum, add as needed)

□ 7. Use policy conditions (IP restrictions, HTTPS only, MFA)

□ 8. Monitor with CloudTrail (audit all API calls)

□ 9. Use IAM Access Analyzer (identify unused permissions)

□ 10. Never share access keys across teams/environments
```

### 6. Common IAM Mistakes & Solutions

| Mistake | Solution |
|---------|----------|
| Giving `"Resource": "*"` to everyone | Specify exact ARNs |
| Using root user for daily tasks | Create IAM admin user |
| Hardcoding keys in code | Use IAM roles |
| Shared keys across team | Individual users + groups |
| No MFA for privileged users | Enable MFA + policy requiring MFA |
| Too permissive (S3FullAccess) | Granular actions (s3:GetObject only) |

### 7. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"IAM is the foundation of AWS security. In my project, we used IAM roles for EC2 instances to access S3 and DynamoDB. No hardcoded credentials. We also enforced MFA for console access and used SCPs (Service Control Policies) at the organization level to prevent deletion of CloudTrail logs. For cross-account access, we used role assumption with external ID to prevent confused deputy attacks."*

**Common mistakes:**

- Confusing authentication (who) vs authorization (what)
- Not understanding that DENY overrides ALLOW
- Forgetting IAM is global (not region-specific)
- Not knowing the difference between identity-based and resource-based policies

### 8. Follow-up Questions

| Question | Answer |
|----------|--------|
| *"What's the difference between IAM role and IAM user?"* | User = long-term credentials, Role = temporary credentials via STS |
| *"How do you grant an EC2 instance access to an S3 bucket?"* | Create IAM role with S3 permissions, attach to EC2 instance profile |
| *"What happens if you have both Allow and Deny policies?"* | Explicit DENY always wins |
| *"How long do temporary credentials last?"* | 15 minutes to 36 hours (default 1 hour) |
| *"What's a service control policy (SCP)?"* | Permission guardrails at AWS Organization level (cannot be overridden) |

### 9. Short Summary

```
IAM = Identity & Access Management (Global service)

Three core concepts:
- User: Long-term identity (person/application)
- Role: Temporary identity (assumed by services)
- Policy: JSON document defining permissions

Policy Evaluation (Order):
1. Explicit DENY → DENY (immediate)
2. Explicit ALLOW → ALLOW
3. No match → DENY (implicit)

Best Practices:
- Never use root user
- Always use IAM roles for EC2/Lambda
- Least privilege principle
- Enable MFA for all users
- Rotate keys every 90 days

Common Pattern:
EC2 → IAM Role (trusts EC2 service) → Policy (allows S3 read)
→ Instance profile attached at launch
→ SDK automatically gets credentials

Interview Hook: 
"IAM is about who can do what. The most important concept is 
that explicit DENY overrides any ALLOW. I always follow least 
privilege and use roles instead of users for services."
```

---

## ❓ Q5. What is VPC?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
VPC = Virtual Private Cloud. Your own private network inside AWS. Like having your own data center but in the cloud.

**Intermediate:**  
VPC is a logically isolated section of AWS where you can launch resources in a virtual network you define. Complete control over IP addresses, subnets, routing, and security.

**Advanced (Internal Working):**

```
VPC Architecture (Deep Dive):
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                        Region                               │
│  ┌───────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐  │
│  │                      VPC (10.0.0.0/16)                │  │
│  │  ┌─────────────────┐        ┌─────────────────┐      │  │
│  │  │   Public AZ1    │        │   Public AZ2    │      │  │
│  │  │  Subnet A       │        │  Subnet B       │      │  │
│  │  │ 10.0.1.0/24     │        │ 10.0.2.0/24     │      │  │
│  │  │                 │        │                 │      │  │
│  │  │  ┌─────────┐    │        │  ┌─────────┐    │      │  │
│  │  │  │  ALB    │    │        │  │  ALB    │    │      │  │
│  │  │  └────┬────┘    │        │  └────┬────┘    │      │  │
│  │  │  ┌────┴────┐    │        │  ┌────┴────┐    │      │  │
│  │  │  │  EC2    │    │        │  │  EC2    │    │      │  │
│  │  │  └─────────┘    │        │  └─────────┘    │      │  │
│  │  └─────────────────┘        └─────────────────┘      │  │
│  │                                                       │  │
│  │  ┌─────────────────┐        ┌─────────────────┐      │  │
│  │  │  Private AZ1    │        │  Private AZ2    │      │  │
│  │  │  Subnet C       │        │  Subnet D       │      │  │
│  │  │ 10.0.3.0/24     │        │ 10.0.4.0/24     │      │  │
│  │  │                 │        │                 │      │  │
│  │  │  ┌─────────┐    │        │  ┌─────────┐    │      │  │
│  │  │  │  RDS    │    │        │  │  RDS    │    │      │  │
│  │  │  │(Primary)│    │        │  │(Standby)│    │      │  │
│  │  │  └─────────┘    │        │  └─────────┘    │      │  │
│  │  └─────────────────┘        └─────────────────┘      │  │
│  └───────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘  │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 2. VPC Components (Deep Dive)

**Component 1: CIDR Blocks (IP Address Range)**

```
CIDR Notation Explained:
10.0.0.0/16
│       │
│       └── Subnet mask (bits)
└── Base IP

CIDR Ranges:
/16 = 65,536 IPs   (10.0.0.0 to 10.0.255.255)
/24 = 256 IPs      (10.0.1.0 to 10.0.1.255)
/28 = 16 IPs       (10.0.1.0 to 10.0.1.15)

Reserved IPs (AWS uses 5 per subnet):
- Network address (10.0.1.0)
- VPC router (10.0.1.1)
- DNS server (10.0.1.2)
- Future use (10.0.1.3)
- Broadcast (10.0.1.255) - not used in VPC

Available for your resources: 10.0.1.4 to 10.0.1.254 (251 IPs)
```

**Component 2: Subnets (Public vs Private)**

```
Public Subnet:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ Has direct route to Internet Gateway (IGW)                 │
│ - Resources have public IPs (auto-assign enabled)          │
│ - Can be accessed from internet                            │
│ - Use for: Load balancers, NAT gateways, bastion hosts     │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Private Subnet:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ No direct route to IGW                                      │
│ - Resources have private IPs only                           │
│ - Cannot be accessed from internet                          │
│ - Can access internet via NAT Gateway (outbound only)       │
│ - Use for: Application servers, databases                   │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Component 3: Internet Gateway (IGW)**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                   Internet Gateway                          │
│                                                             │
│  Purpose: Bridge between VPC and internet                   │
│  - 1:1 relationship with VPC (1 VPC = 1 IGW max)           │
│  - Highly available (AWS managed)                          │
│  - Performs NAT (Network Address Translation)              │
│                                                             │
│  How it works:                                             │
│  Internet ←→ IGW ←→ VPC Route Table ←→ Subnet ←→ EC2       │
│                                                             │
│  Route table entry:                                        │
│  Destination: 0.0.0.0/0 (any internet IP)                 │
│  Target: igw-12345 (Internet Gateway)                      │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Component 4: NAT Gateway (Network Address Translation)**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                      NAT Gateway                            │
│                                                             │
│  Purpose: Allow private subnets to access internet         │
│           (but NOT allow internet to access private)       │
│                                                             │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │ Private EC2 (10.0.3.5) wants to download package    │   │
│  │                    │                                 │   │
│  │                    ▼                                 │   │
│  │ Route table: 0.0.0.0/0 → NAT Gateway (10.0.1.10)   │   │
│  │                    │                                 │   │
│  │                    ▼                                 │   │
│  │ NAT Gateway (in public subnet)                      │   │
│  │ - Changes source IP from 10.0.3.5 to its public IP │   │
│  │ - Sends to internet                                 │   │
│  │ - Response comes back → reverse NAT                 │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                             │
│  NAT Gateway vs NAT Instance:                              │
│  - NAT Gateway: Managed, HA, scalable (cost per hour)      │
│  - NAT Instance: Self-managed EC2, cheaper but single AZ   │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

**Component 5: Security Groups vs NACL (Deep Comparison)**

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│            SECURITY GROUP (Instance-level)                  │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│ Stateful:                                                   │
│ - If you allow inbound, outbound auto-allowed              │
│ - Example: Allow inbound on port 80 → Outbound on port     │
│   80 automatically allowed for response                     │
│                                                             │
│ Rules:                                                      │
│ - Only ALLOW rules (no DENY)                               │
│ - Evaluated together (ALL if any matches)                  │
│ - Processed in order (but all considered)                  │
│                                                             │
│ Scope:                                                      │
│ - Attached to ENI (Elastic Network Interface)              │
│ - Can reference other security groups                      │
│                                                             │
│ Example:                                                    │
│ Allow HTTP from ALB security group                         │
│ Allow SSH from bastion security group                      │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    NACL (Subnet-level)                      │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│ Stateless:                                                  │
│ - Need separate inbound AND outbound rules                 │
│ - Example: Allow inbound on 80 → MUST also allow           │
│   outbound on ephemeral ports (1024-65535)                 │
│                                                             │
│ Rules:                                                      │
│ - Can have ALLOW and DENY rules                            │
│ - Evaluated in order (rule number)                         │
│ - First match wins                                          │
│                                                             │
│ Scope:                                                      │
│ - Attached to subnet                                       │
│ - Affects all resources in subnet                          │
│                                                             │
│ Example:                                                    │
│ 100: Allow HTTP from 0.0.0.0/0                             │
│ 200: Allow SSH from office IP                              │
│ 300: DENY all from malicious IP                            │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 3. VPC Flow Diagram (Complete)

```
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │           Internet                      │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                      │
                                      ▼
                    ┌─────────────────────────────────────────┐
                    │        Internet Gateway (IGW)           │
                    └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                      │
                    ┌─────────────────┴───────────────────────┐
                    │                                         │
                    ▼                                         ▼
        ┌─────────────────────┐                   ┌─────────────────────┐
        │   Public Subnet AZ1 │                   │   Public Subnet AZ2 │
        │    10.0.1.0/24      │                   │    10.0.2.0/24      │
        │                     │                   │                     │
        │  ┌───────────────┐  │                   │  ┌───────────────┐  │
        │  │  NAT Gateway  │  │                   │  │  NAT Gateway  │  │
        │  │ 10.0.1.10     │  │                   │  │ 10.0.2.10     │  │
        │  └───────┬───────┘  │                   │  └───────┬───────┘  │
        │          │          │                   │          │          │
        │  ┌───────┴───────┐  │                   │  ┌───────┴───────┐  │
        │  │     ALB       │  │                   │  │     ALB       │  │
        │  │  10.0.1.20    │  │                   │  │  10.0.2.20    │  │
        │  └───────┬───────┘  │                   │  └───────┬───────┘  │
        └──────────┼──────────┘                   └──────────┼──────────┘
                   │                                         │
                   └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                     │
                    ┌────────────────┴───────────────────────┐
                    │                                         │
                    ▼                                         ▼
        ┌─────────────────────┐                   ┌─────────────────────┐
        │  Private Subnet AZ1 │                   │  Private Subnet AZ2 │
        │    10.0.3.0/24      │                   │    10.0.4.0/24      │
        │                     │                   │                     │
        │  ┌───────────────┐  │                   │  ┌───────────────┐  │
        │  │     EC2-1     │  │                   │  │     EC2-2     │  │
        │  │    App Server │  │                   │  │    App Server │  │
        │  │   10.0.3.5    │  │                   │  │   10.0.4.5    │  │
        │  └───────┬───────┘  │                   │  └───────┬───────┘  │
        │          │          │                   │          │          │
        │  ┌───────┴───────┐  │                   │  ┌───────┴───────┐  │
        │  │     RDS       │  │                   │  │     RDS       │  │
        │  │   (Primary)   │  │                   │  │   (Standby)   │  │
        │  │   10.0.3.20   │  │                   │  │   10.0.4.20   │  │
        │  └───────────────┘  │                   │  └───────────────┘  │
        └─────────────────────┘                   └─────────────────────┘
                   │                                         │
                   └─────────────────┬───────────────────────┘
                                     │
                              (Synchronous Replication)
```

### 4. VPC Peering vs Transit Gateway vs VPN

```
VPC Peering:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ VPC A (10.0.0.0/16) ←───── Peering ─────→ VPC B (10.1.0.0/16)
│                                                             │
│ Limitations:                                               │
│ - No transitive peering (A to B, B to C doesn't give A to C)│
│ - No overlapping CIDR blocks                               │
│ - Max 125 peerings per VPC                                 │
│ - Not supported across regions? Actually yes (inter-region)│
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

Transit Gateway (Hub-and-Spoke):
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    Transit Gateway                          │
│                          │                                  │
│        ┌─────────────────┼─────────────────┐               │
│        │                 │                 │               │
│        ▼                 ▼                 ▼               │
│     VPC A             VPC B             VPC C              │
│   (10.0.0.0/16)    (10.1.0.0/16)    (10.2.0.0/16)         │
│                                                             │
│ Benefits:                                                  │
│ - Transitive routing                                       │
│ - Connect thousands of VPCs                                │
│ - Hybrid connectivity (VPN, Direct Connect)                │
│ - Cross-region peering                                     │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘

VPN Connection (to on-premises):
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│ On-Premises Data Center    ←── VPN (IPsec) ──→   AWS VPC    │
│   10.0.0.0/8                     │               10.1.0.0/16│
│                                  │                          │
│                          Virtual Private Gateway            │
│                          (VGW) attached to VPC              │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 5. VPC Security Best Practices

```
VPC Security Checklist:

□ 1. Use multiple AZs (minimum 2 for production)

□ 2. Put databases in private subnets (no direct internet access)

□ 3. Use NAT Gateway for private instances to access internet

□ 4. Security Groups: Least privilege, specific ports only

□ 5. NACLs: Add explicit DENY for bad actors

□ 6. VPC Flow Logs: Enable for troubleshooting and security

□ 7. Use VPC endpoints for S3/DynamoDB (keep traffic within AWS)

□ 8. Never put RDS in public subnet

□ 9. Use bastion host (jump box) for SSH access to private instances

□ 10. Implement VPC peering instead of exposing services to internet
```

### 6. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"VPC is your private network in AWS. In my last project, we designed a multi-AZ VPC with public subnets for load balancers and NAT gateways, and private subnets for application servers and RDS databases. We used security groups at the instance level and NACLs at the subnet level. For database access, we used VPC peering between the application VPC and data warehouse VPC. We enabled VPC Flow Logs to troubleshoot connectivity issues and detect anomalies."*

**Common mistakes:**

- Putting databases in public subnets
- Forgetting NACLs are stateless (need both inbound/outbound)
- Overlapping CIDR blocks in peered VPCs
- Not planning IP space for future growth
- Using /16 VPC when /24 is enough (waste of IPs)

### 7. Follow-up Questions

| Question | Answer |
|----------|--------|
| *"Can you change VPC CIDR after creation?"* | No, delete and recreate. Plan carefully! |
| *"What's the difference between security group and NACL?"* | SG = instance-level, stateful. NACL = subnet-level, stateless. |
| *"How do you connect VPC to on-premises?"* | VPN (IPsec) or Direct Connect (dedicated fiber) |
| *"What are VPC endpoints?"* | Private connection to AWS services (S3, DynamoDB) without internet |
| *"How many subnets can a VPC have?"* | 200 per VPC (can increase) |

### 8. Short Summary

```
VPC = Virtual Private Cloud (logically isolated network)

Core Components:
- CIDR Blocks (IP range, e.g., 10.0.0.0/16)
- Subnets (Public/Private, tied to AZ)
- Internet Gateway (public internet access)
- NAT Gateway (outbound internet for private)
- Route Tables (traffic routing rules)
- Security Groups (instance firewall, stateful)
- NACLs (subnet firewall, stateless)
- VPC Peering (connect VPCs)
- VPC Endpoints (private access to AWS services)

Best Practice Architecture:
Public Subnet: ALB, NAT Gateway, Bastion
Private Subnet: EC2, RDS, Redis
Multi-AZ for high availability

Interview Hook:
"VPC is where you define your network perimeter. 
The key is to put only what needs internet in public subnets, 
everything else in private. Always use multiple AZs and enable 
Flow Logs for visibility."
```

- Highly available system design
- Stateless vs Stateful
