# 🔐 5. SECURITY (Important for Senior Roles)

## ❓ Q17. How do you secure your backend?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
Backend secure karne ke liye:
- **Authentication** (who are you?) - Login, JWT tokens
- **Authorization** (what can you do?) - IAM roles, permissions
- **Encryption** (data protect) - HTTPS, encrypted storage
- **Network security** - Firewalls, VPC

**Intermediate:**  
Security = **Defense in depth** - multiple layers of security so if one layer fails, others still protect.

**Advanced (Defense in Depth - All Layers):**

```
DEFENSE IN DEPTH - COMPLETE SECURITY ARCHITECTURE:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                                                                             │
│  LAYER 1: PHYSICAL SECURITY (AWS manages)                                  │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  - 24/7 surveillance, biometric access, armed guards                │   │
│  │  - AWS data centers (no public access)                              │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  LAYER 2: NETWORK SECURITY (VPC, Subnets, Firewalls)                       │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Internet ──→ CloudFront (CDN) ──→ WAF ──→ ALB                      │   │
│  │                                              │                        │   │
│  │                                              ▼                        │   │
│  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │   │
│  │  │  Public Subnet (ALB, NAT Gateway)                           │   │   │
│  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │   │
│  │                              │                                      │   │
│  │                              ▼                                      │   │
│  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │   │
│  │  │  Private Subnet (EC2, RDS)                                  │   │   │
│  │  │  - Security Groups (instance-level firewall)                │   │   │
│  │  │  - NACLs (subnet-level firewall)                            │   │   │
│  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  LAYER 3: IDENTITY & ACCESS MANAGEMENT (IAM)                               │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  - IAM Roles for EC2/Lambda (no hardcoded keys)                    │   │
│  │  - Least privilege (minimum permissions)                            │   │
│  │  - MFA for all users                                                │   │
│  │  - Regular key rotation (every 90 days)                             │   │
│  │  - AWS SSO for human users                                          │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  LAYER 4: DATA SECURITY (Encryption)                                       │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  In Transit (TLS 1.3):                                              │   │
│  │  - HTTPS for all APIs                                               │   │
│  │  - SSL/TLS for RDS, Redis                                          │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  At Rest:                                                           │   │
│  │  - RDS: AWS KMS encryption                                          │   │
│  │  - S3: SSE-S3 or SSE-KMS                                            │   │
│  │  - EBS: Encryption enabled                                          │   │
│  │  - Secrets Manager for credentials                                  │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  LAYER 5: APPLICATION SECURITY                                              │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  - Input validation (prevent SQL injection, XSS)                    │   │
│  │  - Rate limiting (API Gateway)                                      │   │
│  │  - JWT validation (stateless auth)                                  │   │
│  │  - WAF rules (SQL injection, XSS, bad bots)                         │   │
│  │  - AWS Shield (DDoS protection)                                     │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  LAYER 6: MONITORING & AUDITING                                            │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  - CloudTrail (API audit log)                                       │   │
│  │  - CloudWatch (metrics + logs)                                      │   │
│  │  - GuardDuty (threat detection)                                     │   │
│  │  - Security Hub (compliance)                                        │   │
│  │  - AWS Config (resource compliance)                                 │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 2. Complete Security Checklist

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    BACKEND SECURITY - COMPLETE CHECKLIST                    │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                             │
│  ☐ NETWORK SECURITY                                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  □ VPC with public/private subnets                                  │   │
│  │  □ Security Groups (least privilege, specific ports)                │   │
│  │  □ NACLs (explicit DENY for bad actors)                             │   │
│  │  □ No public IPs for databases                                      │   │
│  │  □ VPC endpoints for S3/DynamoDB (no internet)                      │   │
│  │  □ WAF enabled (SQL injection, XSS protection)                      │   │
│  │  □ AWS Shield Advanced (DDoS protection)                            │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  ☐ IDENTITY & ACCESS                                                       │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  □ IAM roles for EC2/Lambda (no access keys on instances)           │   │
│  │  □ Least privilege (only required permissions)                      │   │
│  │  □ MFA for all IAM users                                            │   │
│  │  □ Regular access key rotation (90 days)                            │   │
│  │  □ Delete unused IAM users/roles                                    │   │
│  │  □ Use IAM groups (not individual user policies)                    │   │
│  │  □ API Gateway with Cognito or Lambda authorizer                    │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  ☐ DATA ENCRYPTION                                                         │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  □ TLS 1.3 for all APIs (HTTPS)                                     │   │
│  │  □ RDS encryption at rest (KMS)                                     │   │
│  │  □ S3 encryption (SSE-S3 or SSE-KMS)                                │   │
│  │  □ EBS encryption enabled                                           │   │
│  │  □ Secrets Manager for DB passwords, API keys                       │   │
│  │  □ Parameter Store for configuration                                │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  ☐ APPLICATION SECURITY                                                    │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  □ Input validation (whitelist approach)                            │   │
│  │  □ Parameterized queries (SQL injection prevention)                 │   │
│  │  □ Output encoding (XSS prevention)                                 │   │
│  │  □ Rate limiting (prevent abuse)                                    │   │
│  │  □ JWT with short expiry (15-60 minutes)                            │   │
│  │  □ CSRF protection (tokens)                                         │   │
│  │  □ Security headers (CSP, HSTS, X-Frame-Options)                    │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  ☐ MONITORING & AUDITING                                                   │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  □ CloudTrail enabled (all regions)                                 │   │
│  │  □ CloudWatch logs for all services                                 │   │
│  │  □ GuardDuty enabled (threat detection)                             │   │
│  │  □ Security Hub enabled (compliance)                                │   │
│  │  □ Alarms for suspicious activity                                   │   │
│  │  □ Regular security audits                                          │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  ☐ COMPLIANCE                                                              │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  □ GDPR compliance (if EU users)                                    │   │
│  │  □ HIPAA compliance (if healthcare)                                 │   │
│  │  □ PCI DSS compliance (if payments)                                 │   │
│  │  □ SOC 2 compliance                                                 │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 3. Real-World Security Implementation (Code Examples)

```java
// 1. JWT Authentication with short expiry
@Configuration
public class JwtConfig {
    
    @Bean
    public JwtDecoder jwtDecoder() {
        // JWT expires in 15 minutes
        return NimbusJwtDecoder.withJwkSetUri("https://cognito-idp.{region}.amazonaws.com/{userPoolId}/.well-known/jwks.json")
            .build();
    }
}

// 2. Input Validation (Whitelist approach)
@RestController
public class UserController {
    
    @PostMapping("/users")
    public User createUser(@Valid @RequestBody CreateUserRequest request) {
        // @Valid triggers validation
        return userService.createUser(request);
    }
}

public class CreateUserRequest {
    
    @NotBlank(message = "Email is required")
    @Email(message = "Invalid email format")
    @Size(max = 100, message = "Email too long")
    private String email;
    
    @NotBlank(message = "Name is required")
    @Pattern(regexp = "^[a-zA-Z\\s]+$", message = "Name can only contain letters")
    @Size(min = 2, max = 50, message = "Name must be 2-50 characters")
    private String name;
    
    // Password: min 8 chars, 1 uppercase, 1 number, 1 special
    @NotBlank
    @Pattern(regexp = "^(?=.*[A-Z])(?=.*\\d)(?=.*[@$!%*?&])[A-Za-z\\d@$!%*?&]{8,}$")
    private String password;
}

// 3. Parameterized Query (SQL Injection prevention)
@Repository
public class UserRepository {
    
    // GOOD: Parameterized query
    @Query("SELECT u FROM User u WHERE u.email = :email")
    User findByEmail(@Param("email") String email);
    
    // BAD: String concatenation (NEVER do this!)
    // @Query("SELECT u FROM User u WHERE u.email = '" + email + "'")
}

// 4. Rate Limiting
@Configuration
public class RateLimitingConfig {
    
    @Bean
    public RedisRateLimiter rateLimiter() {
        return new RedisRateLimiter(10, 20); // 10 requests per second, burst 20
    }
}

// 5. Security Headers
@Configuration
public class SecurityHeadersConfig {
    
    @Bean
    public FilterRegistrationBean<HeaderWriterFilter> securityHeadersFilter() {
        HeaderWriterFilter filter = new HeaderWriterFilter(
            new XContentTypeOptionsHeaderWriter(),
            new XXssProtectionHeaderWriter(),
            new CacheControlHeadersWriter(),
            new HstsHeaderWriter(), // HTTP Strict Transport Security
            new ContentSecurityPolicyHeaderWriter("default-src 'self'")
        );
        // ... register filter
    }
}
```

### 4. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"Security is defense in depth. I start with network security - VPC with private subnets for databases, security groups that only allow necessary ports. For identity, I use IAM roles for EC2 - no hardcoded keys. All data is encrypted at rest (RDS, S3, EBS) and in transit (TLS 1.3). For authentication, we use JWT with 15-minute expiry and refresh tokens. WAF protects against SQL injection and XSS. We monitor everything with CloudTrail, GuardDuty, and have alerts for suspicious activity. Regular security audits and penetration testing are mandatory."*

**Key points to mention:**

1. **Defense in depth** - Multiple layers
2. **Least privilege** - Minimum permissions
3. **Encrypt everything** - At rest and in transit
4. **No hardcoded secrets** - Use Secrets Manager
5. **Monitor everything** - CloudTrail, GuardDuty
6. **Regular audits** - Penetration testing

---

## ❓ Q18. What is Security Group vs NACL?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
- **Security Group** = Instance-level firewall (EC2 ke level pe)
- **NACL** = Subnet-level firewall (poori subnet ke liye)

**Advanced (Deep Comparison):**

```
SECURITY GROUP vs NACL - VISUAL COMPARISON:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                                                                             │
│  VPC (10.0.0.0/16)                                                         │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Public Subnet (10.0.1.0/24)                                        │   │
│  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │   │
│  │  │  NACL (Subnet-level) - Stateless                            │   │   │
│  │  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  Rule 100: Allow HTTP from 0.0.0.0/0 (inbound)      │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  Rule 110: Allow HTTPS from 0.0.0.0/0 (inbound)     │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  Rule 200: Allow SSH from office IP (inbound)       │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  Rule 300: DENY all from malicious IP (inbound)     │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │                                                      │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  Rule 100: Allow ephemeral ports out (outbound)     │   │   │   │
│  │  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │   │   │
│  │  │                           │                                  │   │   │
│  │  │                           ▼                                  │   │   │
│  │  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  EC2 Instance                                        │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  │  Security Group (Instance-level) - Stateful │   │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  │  - Allow HTTP from ALB SG                    │   │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  │  - Allow SSH from Bastion SG                 │   │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  │  - Allow HTTPS from CloudFront SG            │   │   │   │   │
│  │  │  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │   │   │   │
│  │  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │   │   │
│  │  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 2. Detailed Comparison Table

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              SECURITY GROUP vs NACL - COMPLETE COMPARISON                   │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                             │
│  Feature                    | Security Group        | NACL                  │
│  ───────────────────────────┼───────────────────────┼───────────────────────│
│  **Level**                  | Instance (ENI) level  | Subnet level          │
│  **Stateful?**              | YES                   | NO                    │
│  **Rules**                  | ALLOW only            | ALLOW and DENY        │
│  **Rule Evaluation**        | All rules evaluated   | Ordered (first match) │
│  **Default Rules**          | Deny all inbound,     | Allow all inbound/    │
│                            | Allow all outbound    | outbound              │
│  **Attach to**              | EC2, RDS, ELB, Lambda | Subnet only           │
│  **Rule Limit**             | 60 inbound + 60       | 20 rules per direction│
│                            | outbound (default)    | (can increase)        │
│  **Target by**              | IP, CIDR, SG          | IP, CIDR only         │
│  **Use Case**               | Instance firewall     | Subnet boundary       │
│                                                                             │
│  STATEFUL vs STATELESS (Most Important):                                   │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Security Group (Stateful):                                         │   │
│  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │   │
│  │  │  Inbound: Allow HTTP (port 80) from 0.0.0.0/0              │    │   │
│  │  │                                                             │    │   │
│  │  │  Result: Outbound automatically allowed (no rule needed)   │    │   │
│  │  │  - Response traffic is automatically permitted             │    │   │
│  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  NACL (Stateless):                                                  │   │
│  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │   │
│  │  │  Inbound: Allow HTTP (port 80) from 0.0.0.0/0              │    │   │
│  │  │                                                             │    │   │
│  │  │  Outbound: MUST ALSO allow ephemeral ports (1024-65535)    │    │   │
│  │  │  - Response traffic is NOT automatically allowed           │    │   │
│  │  │  - Need explicit outbound rule                             │    │   │
│  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 3. Real-World Configuration Examples

```json
// SECURITY GROUP (AWS Console / CloudFormation)
{
  "SecurityGroup": {
    "GroupName": "web-server-sg",
    "Description": "Security group for web servers",
    "VpcId": "vpc-12345",
    "SecurityGroupIngress": [
      {
        "Description": "HTTP from ALB",
        "IpProtocol": "tcp",
        "FromPort": 80,
        "ToPort": 80,
        "SourceSecurityGroupId": "sg-alb123"  // Reference by SG, not IP
      },
      {
        "Description": "HTTPS from CloudFront",
        "IpProtocol": "tcp",
        "FromPort": 443,
        "ToPort": 443,
        "SourceSecurityGroupId": "sg-cloudfront123"
      },
      {
        "Description": "SSH from Bastion",
        "IpProtocol": "tcp",
        "FromPort": 22,
        "ToPort": 22,
        "SourceSecurityGroupId": "sg-bastion123"
      }
    ],
    "SecurityGroupEgress": [
      {
        "Description": "Outbound internet",
        "IpProtocol": "tcp",
        "FromPort": 443,
        "ToPort": 443,
        "CidrIp": "0.0.0.0/0"
      }
    ]
  }
}

// NACL (Subnet-level, ordered rules)
{
  "NetworkAcl": {
    "Entries": [
      // Inbound Rules (evaluated in order, first match wins)
      {
        "RuleNumber": 100,
        "Protocol": "tcp",
        "RuleAction": "allow",
        "CidrBlock": "0.0.0.0/0",
        "PortRange": {"From": 80, "To": 80}
      },
      {
        "RuleNumber": 110,
        "Protocol": "tcp",
        "RuleAction": "allow",
        "CidrBlock": "0.0.0.0/0",
        "PortRange": {"From": 443, "To": 443}
      },
      {
        "RuleNumber": 200,
        "Protocol": "tcp",
        "RuleAction": "allow",
        "CidrBlock": "203.0.113.0/24",  // Office IP
        "PortRange": {"From": 22, "To": 22}
      },
      {
        "RuleNumber": 300,
        "Protocol": "tcp",
        "RuleAction": "deny",
        "CidrBlock": "198.51.100.0/24",  // Malicious IP range
        "PortRange": {"From": 0, "To": 65535}
      },
      {
        "RuleNumber": 32767,
        "Protocol": "tcp",
        "RuleAction": "deny",
        "CidrBlock": "0.0.0.0/0",
        "PortRange": {"From": 0, "To": 65535}
      },
      
      // Outbound Rules (MUST allow ephemeral ports for responses!)
      {
        "RuleNumber": 100,
        "Protocol": "tcp",
        "RuleAction": "allow",
        "CidrBlock": "0.0.0.0/0",
        "PortRange": {"From": 1024, "To": 65535}  // Ephemeral ports
      }
    ]
  }
}
```

### 4. Best Practices & Common Mistakes

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│              SECURITY GROUP & NACL - BEST PRACTICES                         │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                             │
│  BEST PRACTICES:                                                           │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  1. Use Security Groups by default (simpler, stateful)              │   │
│  │  2. Reference other Security Groups, not IPs (when possible)        │   │
│  │  3. Apply least privilege (only needed ports)                       │   │
│  │  4. Use NACLs for additional DENY rules (bad IPs)                   │   │
│  │  5. Enable VPC Flow Logs to verify rules                           │   │
│  │  6. Regularly audit unused rules                                    │   │
│  │  7. Use descriptive names and tags                                  │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  COMMON MISTAKES:                                                          │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  ❌ Opening 0.0.0.0/0 for SSH (use bastion host)                    │   │
│  │  ❌ Forgetting NACL outbound rules (stateless!)                      │   │
│  │  ❌ Using overlapping rules (order matters in NACL)                 │   │
│  │  ❌ Not removing unused security groups                              │   │
│  │  ❌ Using IPs instead of security group references                   │   │
│  │  ❌ Allowing all outbound traffic (0.0.0.0/0) without need          │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 5. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"Security groups are stateful, instance-level firewalls. I use them as my primary defense - they're simpler because response traffic is auto-allowed. NACLs are stateless, subnet-level firewalls. I use NACLs for additional DENY rules, like blocking known malicious IP ranges. The key difference is stateful vs stateless - with NACLs, you need both inbound AND outbound rules. For example, allowing HTTP inbound requires also allowing ephemeral ports outbound for the response."*

### 6. Short Summary

```
Security Group vs NACL - Quick Reference:

Security Group:
- Level: Instance
- Stateful: YES (auto allow responses)
- Rules: ALLOW only
- Order: All rules evaluated
- Default: Deny all inbound, allow all outbound

NACL:
- Level: Subnet
- Stateful: NO (need both directions)
- Rules: ALLOW and DENY
- Order: First match wins (rule numbers)
- Default: Allow all (custom VPC)

Best Practice:
- Use Security Groups for 90% of rules
- Use NACLs for explicit DENY (bad IPs)
- Never use NACLs without understanding stateless!

Interview Hook:
"Security groups are my go-to - they're stateful and simple. 
I only use NACLs when I need explicit DENY rules or 
subnet-level protection. The stateless nature of NACLs 
catches many people off guard."
```

---

## ❓ Q19. How to store secrets securely?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
Secrets (passwords, API keys) ko kabhi **code me hardcode** nahi karna. Use **AWS Secrets Manager** or **Parameter Store**.

**Advanced (Complete Secrets Management):**

```
SECRETS MANAGEMENT ARCHITECTURE:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                                                                             │
│  DEVELOPMENT WORKFLOW:                                                      │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Developer writes code:                                              │   │
│  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │   │
│  │  │  // NEVER do this!                                           │    │   │
│  │  │  String password = "MySecret123!";  // ❌ Hardcoded          │    │   │
│  │  │                                                             │    │   │
│  │  │  // DO this:                                                │    │   │
│  │  │  String password = secretsManager.getSecret("db/password"); │    │   │
│  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                    AWS SECRETS STORAGE                               │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Option 1: AWS Secrets Manager ($0.40 per secret/month)             │   │
│  │  - Automatic rotation (RDS credentials)                             │   │
│  │  - Cross-account access                                             │   │
│  │  - Audit logging (CloudTrail)                                       │   │
│  │  - Best for: Database passwords, API keys                           │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Option 2: AWS Systems Manager Parameter Store (Free tier)          │   │
│  │  - SecureString with KMS encryption                                 │   │
│  │  - Hierarchical paths (/prod/db/password)                          │   │
│  │  - TTL support                                                      │   │
│  │  - Best for: Configuration, non-sensitive (free)                    │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Option 3: AWS KMS (Key Management Service)                         │   │
│  │  - Encryption keys only (not for secrets)                           │   │
│  │  - Used by Secrets Manager and Parameter Store                       │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                    APPLICATION ACCESS                                │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  EC2 Instance:                                                       │   │
│  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │   │
│  │  │  IAM Role attached to EC2 (no hardcoded keys!)              │    │   │
│  │  │  - Policy allows secretsmanager:GetSecretValue              │    │   │
│  │  │  - SDK automatically gets credentials from metadata         │    │   │
│  │  │  - No AWS keys in code!                                     │    │   │
│  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Lambda Function:                                                   │   │
│  │  ┌────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐    │   │
│  │  │  IAM Role attached to Lambda                                 │    │   │
│  │  │  - Cache secrets in memory (reduce calls)                   │    │   │
│  │  │  - Use extension for automatic refresh                      │    │   │
│  │  └────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘    │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 2. Code Examples

```java
// AWS Secrets Manager - Java Example
@Service
public class SecretsManagerService {
    
    private final SecretsManagerClient secretsClient;
    private final Map<String, String> cache = new ConcurrentHashMap<>();
    
    public String getSecret(String secretName) {
        // Check cache first
        if (cache.containsKey(secretName)) {
            return cache.get(secretName);
        }
        
        // Fetch from Secrets Manager
        GetSecretValueRequest request = GetSecretValueRequest.builder()
            .secretId(secretName)
            .build();
        
        GetSecretValueResponse response = secretsClient.getSecretValue(request);
        String secret = response.secretString();
        
        // Cache for 5 minutes (reduce API calls)
        cache.put(secretName, secret);
        ScheduledExecutorService executor = Executors.newSingleThreadScheduledExecutor();
        executor.schedule(() -> cache.remove(secretName), 5, TimeUnit.MINUTES);
        
        return secret;
    }
}

// Spring Boot - Using Parameter Store
@Configuration
public class AppConfig {
    
    @Value("${db.password}")  // Comes from Parameter Store
    private String dbPassword;
    
    // Or use @ConfigurationProperties
    @Bean
    @ConfigurationProperties(prefix = "app")
    public AppProperties appProperties() {
        return new AppProperties();
    }
}

// application.yml (with Spring Cloud AWS)
spring:
  cloud:
    aws:
      parameterstore:
        enabled: true
        prefix: /config
  config:
    import: aws-parameterstore:

// Now values from Parameter Store are automatically injected
// /config/prod/db/password → ${db.password}
```

### 3. Secrets Manager vs Parameter Store

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│           SECRETS MANAGER vs PARAMETER STORE - COMPLETE COMPARISON          │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                             │
│  Feature              | Secrets Manager     | Parameter Store               │
│  ─────────────────────┼─────────────────────┼──────────────────────────────│
│  **Cost**             | $0.40/secret/month  | Free (standard parameters)   │
│                       | + $0.05/10k API     | $0.05/10k API (advanced)     │
│  **Secret size**      | 10KB                | 4KB (standard)               │
│                       |                     | 8KB (advanced)               │
│  **Automatic rotation**| YES (RDS, Redshift) | NO (manual)                  │
│  **Cross-account**    | YES                 | YES (with KMS)               │
│  **Audit logging**    | CloudTrail          | CloudTrail                    │
│  **Versioning**       | YES (automatic)     | YES (manual)                  │
│  **TTL/Expiration**   | YES                 | YES                           │
│  **Use Case**         | Database passwords,  | Config values, non-sensitive │
│                       | API keys, OAuth      | (can be sensitive with       │
│                       | tokens               | SecureString)                │
│                                                                             │
│  When to use which:                                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Use Secrets Manager when:                                          │   │
│  │  - You need automatic rotation (RDS credentials)                    │   │
│  │  - Secret size > 4KB                                                │   │
│  │  - You have budget ($0.40/secret/month is fine)                     │   │
│  │  - You need cross-account access                                    │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Use Parameter Store when:                                          │   │
│  │  - You have many secrets (cost adds up)                             │   │
│  │  - You need hierarchical paths (/prod/db/host)                      │   │
│  │  - You're storing configuration, not just secrets                   │   │
│  │  - You want free tier                                               │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 4. Best Practices

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    SECRETS MANAGEMENT - BEST PRACTICES                      │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                             │
│  ✅ DOs:                                                                   │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  1. Never hardcode secrets in code or config files                  │   │
│  │  2. Use IAM roles for EC2/Lambda (no access keys)                   │   │
│  │  3. Rotate secrets regularly (every 90 days)                        │   │
│  │  4. Use least privilege IAM policies                                │   │
│  │  5. Enable CloudTrail for audit logging                             │   │
│  │  6. Cache secrets (reduce API calls, cost)                          │   │
│  │  7. Use different secrets per environment (dev/staging/prod)        │   │
│  │  8. Encrypt secrets with KMS (customer-managed keys)                │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  ❌ DON'Ts:                                                                │   │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  1. Don't commit secrets to Git (even in private repos)             │   │
│  │  2. Don't log secrets (println, log.debug)                          │   │
│  │  3. Don't pass secrets as environment variables (visible in console)│   │
│  │  4. Don't share secrets across teams (use separate)                 │   │
│  │  5. Don't use default/weak passwords                                │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 5. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"We never hardcode secrets. For database passwords, we use Secrets Manager with automatic rotation every 90 days. For configuration values, we use Parameter Store. EC2 instances have IAM roles with permissions to read only their specific secrets. The code fetches secrets at startup and caches them for 5 minutes to reduce API calls. All access is logged via CloudTrail. This approach has zero hardcoded secrets and automatic rotation."*

### 6. Short Summary

```
Secrets Management - Quick Reference:

NEVER hardcode secrets!

AWS Options:
- Secrets Manager: $0.40/secret, auto rotation
- Parameter Store: Free, manual rotation
- KMS: Encryption keys only

Best Practice Flow:
1. Store secret in Secrets Manager
2. Attach IAM role to EC2/Lambda
3. Code fetches secret at runtime
4. Cache secret (5 min TTL)
5. Rotate every 90 days

Interview Hook:
"If you see hardcoded passwords in code, that's a security incident. 
Always use Secrets Manager or Parameter Store with IAM roles."
```

---

# 📊 6. MONITORING & LOGGING

## ❓ Q20. How do you monitor applications?

### 1. Concept Explanation (Beginner → Advanced)

**Beginner:**  
Monitoring = App ke **health**, **performance**, aur **errors** ko track karna. AWS CloudWatch use karte hain logs, metrics, aur alarms ke liye.

**Advanced (Three Pillars of Observability):**

```
OBSERVABILITY - THREE PILLARS:
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                                                                             │
│  PILLAR 1: METRICS (What happened?)                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  CloudWatch Metrics:                                                │   │
│  │  - CPU Utilization (EC2, RDS)                                       │   │
│  │  - Memory Usage (custom metric)                                     │   │
│  │  - Request count, error rate, latency                               │   │
│  │  - Queue depth (SQS)                                                │   │
│  │  - Database connections                                             │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Custom Metrics (via CloudWatch Agent):                             │   │
│  │  - Business metrics (orders per minute)                             │   │
│  │  - Application-specific (cache hit ratio)                           │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  PILLAR 2: LOGS (Why did it happen?)                                       │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  CloudWatch Logs:                                                   │   │
│  │  - Application logs (log4j, logback)                                │   │
│  │  - Access logs (ALB, CloudFront)                                    │   │
│  │  - System logs (/var/log/messages)                                  │   │
│  │  - Lambda logs (automatically sent)                                 │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Log Insights: Query logs with SQL-like syntax                      │   │
│  │  - "Find all 500 errors in last hour"                               │   │
│  │  - "Group by error message"                                         │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
│  PILLAR 3: TRACING (What path did it take?)                                │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │  AWS X-Ray:                                                         │   │
│  │  - Distributed tracing across services                              │   │
│  │  - Service map (see dependencies)                                   │   │
│  │  - Trace individual requests                                        │   │
│  │  - Identify bottlenecks                                             │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  Example trace:                                                     │   │
│  │  API Gateway (5ms) → Lambda (50ms) → DynamoDB (10ms) → SQS (2ms)   │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 2. Complete Monitoring Architecture

```
┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐
│                    COMPLETE MONITORING ARCHITECTURE                         │
├─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┤
│                                                                             │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                    DATA SOURCES                                      │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  EC2 ──→ CloudWatch Agent ──→ Metrics + Logs                        │   │
│  │  RDS ──→ Enhanced Monitoring ──→ Metrics                            │   │
│  │  ALB ──→ Access Logs ──→ S3 + CloudWatch                           │   │
│  │  Lambda ──→ Built-in ──→ Logs + Metrics + X-Ray                     │   │
│  │  API Gateway ──→ Built-in ──→ Metrics + X-Ray                       │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                    CLOUDWATCH (Central Hub)                          │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  ┌─────────────┐  ┌─────────────┐  ┌─────────────┐                 │   │
│  │  │  Metrics    │  │   Logs      │  │   Alarms    │                 │   │
│  │  │  Dashboard  │  │  Insights   │  │  (SNS)      │                 │   │
│  │  └─────────────┘  └─────────────┘  └─────────────┘                 │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                    │                                        │
│                                    ▼                                        │
│  ┌─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┐   │
│  │                    ACTIONS (Alarms trigger)                          │   │
│  │                                                                      │   │
│  │  SNS Topic ──┬── Email (on-call engineer)                          │   │
│  │              ├── Slack (team channel)                                │   │
│  │              ├── PagerDuty (escalation)                             │   │
│  │              └── Auto Scaling (scale out/in)                        │   │
│  └─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘   │
│                                                                             │
└─────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────────┘
```

### 3. CloudWatch Alarms Configuration

```json
// CloudWatch Alarm Examples

// 1. High CPU Alarm
{
  "AlarmName": "High-CPU-WebServer",
  "MetricName": "CPUUtilization",
  "Namespace": "AWS/EC2",
  "Statistic": "Average",
  "Period": 300,  // 5 minutes
  "EvaluationPeriods": 2,
  "Threshold": 80,
  "ComparisonOperator": "GreaterThanThreshold",
  "ActionsEnabled": true,
  "AlarmActions": ["arn:aws:sns:us-east-1:123456789012:alarm-topic"]
}

// 2. SQS Queue Depth Alarm (for scaling)
{
  "AlarmName": "High-SQS-Queue-Depth",
  "MetricName": "ApproximateNumberOfMessagesVisible",
  "Namespace": "AWS/SQS",
  "Statistic": "Sum",
  "Period": 60,
  "EvaluationPeriods": 2,
  "Threshold": 1000,
  "ComparisonOperator": "GreaterThanThreshold",
  "AlarmActions": ["arn:aws:autoscaling:us-east-1:123456789012:scalingPolicy:..."]
}

// 3. 5xx Error Rate Alarm
{
  "AlarmName": "High-Error-Rate",
  "MetricName": "5xxErrorRate",
  "Namespace": "AWS/ApiGateway",
  "Statistic": "Average",
  "Period": 60,
  "EvaluationPeriods": 2,
  "Threshold": 5,  // 5% error rate
  "ComparisonOperator": "GreaterThanThreshold"
}
```

### 4. Interview Tips

**What interviewers expect:**

✅ *"We use CloudWatch for metrics and logs, and X-Ray for tracing. Key metrics we monitor: CPU, memory, error rate, latency (p99), and SQS queue depth. We have dashboards for at-a-glance health, and alarms for critical conditions - high CPU, high error rate, high queue depth. Alarms trigger SNS to PagerDuty for on-call engineers. We use CloudWatch Log Insights to query logs - 'find all 500 errors in last hour'. X-Ray helps us trace requests across microservices to find bottlenecks."*

### 5. Short Summary

```
Monitoring - Quick Reference:

Three Pillars:
1. Metrics (CloudWatch) - What happened?
2. Logs (CloudWatch Logs) - Why did it happen?
3. Tracing (X-Ray) - What path did it take?

Key Metrics to Monitor:
- CPU > 80% (scale out)
- Memory > 80% (leak or scale)
- Error rate > 5% (alert)
- Latency p99 > 500ms (optimize)
- Queue depth > 1000 (scale workers)

Alarm Actions:
- Email (non-critical)
- Slack (team notification)
- PagerDuty (on-call)
- Auto Scaling (automated)

Interview Hook:
"You can't fix what you can't measure. 
Monitor everything, alert on symptoms (high error rate), 
not causes (high CPU). Use dashboards for trends, 
alarms for anomalies, and traces for debugging."
```
